<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genome sequence of the date palm Phoenix dactylifera L"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2013 4:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2013-08-06"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2013 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Date palm (Phoenix dactylifera L.) is a cultivated woody plant species with agricultural and economic importance. Here we report a genome assembly for an elite variety (Khalas), which is 605.4&#8201;Mb in size and covers &gt;90% of the genome (~671&#8201;Mb) and &gt;96% of its genes (~41,660 genes). Genomic sequence analysis demonstrates that P. dactylifera experienced a clear genome-wide duplication after either ancient whole genome duplications or massive segmental duplications. Genetic diversity analysis indicates that its stress resistance and sugar metabolism-related genes tend to be enriched in the chromosomal regions where the density of single-nucleotide polymorphisms is relatively low. Using transcriptomic data, we also illustrate the date palm&#8217;s unique sugar metabolism that underlies fruit development and ripening. Our large-scale genomic and transcriptomic data pave the way for further genomic studies not only on P. dactylifera but also other Arecaceae plants. The date palm is one of the most economically important plants of the palm family. Here, the authors present a high-quality genome assembly of the date palm Phoenix dactylifera, and reveal insights into the unique sugar metabolism underlying fruit ripening.
                  
                    
                  
                "/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Date palm (<i>Phoenix dactylifera</i> L.) is a cultivated woody plant species with agricultural and economic importance. Here we report a genome assembly for an elite variety (<i>Khalas</i>), which is 605.4 Mb in size and covers &gt;90% of the genome (~671 Mb) and &gt;96% of its genes (~41,660 genes). Genomic sequence analysis demonstrates that <i>P. dactylifera</i> experienced a clear genome-wide duplication after either ancient whole genome duplications or massive segmental duplications. Genetic diversity analysis indicates that its stress resistance and sugar metabolism-related genes tend to be enriched in the chromosomal regions where the density of single-nucleotide polymorphisms is relatively low. Using transcriptomic data, we also illustrate the date palm’s unique sugar metabolism that underlies fruit development and ripening. Our large-scale genomic and transcriptomic data pave the way for further genomic studies not only on <i>P. dactylifera</i> but also other Arecaceae plants.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The economic importance of the palm family plants (Arecaceae or Palmae) is second only to the grass family (Poaceae) among monocotyledons and the third among all plant families after legume (Leguminosae). Among the palm crops, the top three out of the five major domesticated palm species in the world are African oil palm (<i>Elaeis guineensis</i>), coconut (<i>Cocos nucifera</i>) and date palm (<i>Phoenix dactylifera</i>)—the other two are peach palm (<i>Bactris gasipaes</i>) and betel palm (<i>Areca catechu</i>) (FAO, <a href="http://www.fao.org">http://www.fao.org</a>). The economic utility of these palms are multifold, including staple food, beverages, ornamentals, building wood, and industrial materials<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Balick, M. J. &amp; Beck, H. T. Useful Palms of the World: a Synoptic Bibliography Columbia University Press (1990)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d54214e869">1</a></sup>.</p><p><i>P. dactylifera</i> is a strict dioecious evergreen tree capable of living over 100 productive years. It is not only one of the oldest domesticated trees but also of socio-economic importance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Mahmoudi, H., Hosseininia, G., Azadi, H. &amp; Fatemi, M. Enhancing date palm processing, marketing and pest control through organic culture. J. Org. Sys. 3, 29–39 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d54214e878">2</a></sup>. The earliest cultivation of <i>P. dactylifera</i> was recorded in 3,700 BC in the area between the Euphrates and the Nile Rivers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Munier, P. Le palmier-dattier Vol. 24, G P Maisonneuve and Larose (1973)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d54214e885">3</a></sup>. It is thought to be native to the Arabian Peninsula regions, possibly originating from what is now southern Iraq. At a very early time, date palm was introduced by humans to northern India, North Africa, and southern Spain and it has a major economic role in the arid zones<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="El-Juhany, L Degradation of date palm trees and date production in arab countries: causes and potential rehabilitation. Aust. J. Basic. Appl. Sci. 4, 3998–4010 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d54214e889">4</a></sup>. Saudi Arabia, one of the most important countries for date palm cultivation, has &gt;10% of the world’s date palm trees (14% of the date production) and nearly 340 of ~2,000 varieties recorded around the world are grown here<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Al-Maasllem, I. S. Date Palm (Phoenix dactylifera L.) Vol. 7, Encyclopedia Works Publishing &amp; Distribution (1996)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d54214e893">5</a></sup>.</p><p>There have been a very limited number of genome-wide studies on <i>P. dactylifera</i>. One is a recent report on a draft genome assembly based on data generated from the Illumina GAII sequencing platform by a research team in Qatar<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Al-Dous, E. K. et al. De novo genome sequencing and comparative genomics of date palm (Phoenix dactylifera). Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 521–527 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d54214e903">6</a></sup>. They estimated the genome size (658 Mb), assembled 58% of the genome (382 Mb) and predicted 25,059 genes. Another is a comparative transcriptomic study on mesocarps of both oil and date palms based on pyrosequencing data from the Roche GS FLX Titanium platform<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Bourgis, F. et al. Comparative transcriptome and metabolite analysis of oil palm and date palm mesocarp that differ dramatically in carbon partitioning. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 12527–12532 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d54214e907">7</a></sup>. Our effort on <i>P. dactylifera</i> genomics was launched in August 2008 and has delivered full-genome assemblies of its two organelles (158,462 and 715,001 bp for plastid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Yang, M. et al. The complete chloroplast genome sequence of date palm (Phoenix dactylifera L.). PLoS ONE 5, e12762 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d54214e914">8</a></sup> and mitochondrion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Fang, Y. J. et al. A complete sequence and transcriptomic analyses of date palm (Phoenix dactylifera L.) mitochondrial genome. PLoS ONE 7, e37164 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d54214e919">9</a></sup>, respectively). We also constructed transcriptomic profiles for fruit development based on pyrosequencing data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Yin, Y. X. et al. High-throughput sequencing-based gene profiling on multi-staged fruit development of date palm (Phoenix dactylifera, L.). Plant Mol. Biol. 78, 617–626 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d54214e923">10</a></sup>.</p><p>Here, we report our analyses of a more complete <i>P. dactylifera</i> genome assembly in comparison with three other selected varieties, as well as transcriptomic studies on genes related to abiotic resistance and fruit development. The study paves a way for further biological studies on <i>P. dactylifera</i> and comparative genomics for other palm family plants.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly and validation</h3><p>Our core data for contig assembly is composed of 31.4 million high-quality pyrosequencing reads generated from four fragment libraries of the <i>Khalas</i> variety, which cover 17.3 × of the estimated genome length (671.2 Mb, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#MOESM739">Supplementary Note 1</a>) and were assembled into 194,980 contigs (N50=5,806 bp, 507.9 Mb). We further improved the continuity of the Newbler-assembled contigs using SOLiD reads from long mate-pair (LMP) libraries with variable insert sizes (nine libraries and 1–8 kb in length) and mapped ~73.9-Gb sequences uniquely to the contigs (122.1 × ). This intermediate built is composed of 82,863 contigs (N50=195.4 kb; 553.3 Mb). The final assembly was extended based on BAC-end sequences, which contains 82,354 scaffolds (N50=329.9 kb; 558.0 Mb) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#Tab1">Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#MOESM739">Supplementary Table S1</a>). On the basis of our estimated genome size, this assembly (605.4 Mb) covers 90.2% of the genome when contigs &lt;500 bp are also taken into account.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 An overview of the <i>Phoenix dactylifera</i> genome assembly.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ncomms3274/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To assess the coverage and completeness of the genome assembly, we used assembled sequences from 10 BACs (variety <i>Khalas</i>) and six fosmids (from a northern African variety <i>Deglet Noor</i>) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#MOESM739">Supplementary Table S2</a>). The alignments are unambiguous—98.5% and 96.5% for the BACs and the fosmids, respectively—and high-quality with no misalignment (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#MOESM739">Supplementary Figs S1 and S2</a>). We also validated the assembly using ESTs from our own transcriptomic projects<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Zhang, G. Y. et al. Large-scale collection and annotation of gene models for date palm (Phoenix dactylifera, L.). Plant Mol. Biol. 79, 521–536 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1143">11</a></sup>, the Qatar<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Al-Dous, E. K. et al. De novo genome sequencing and comparative genomics of date palm (Phoenix dactylifera). Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 521–527 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1148">6</a></sup> and the CNRS (Centre National de la Recherche Scientifique) group<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Bourgis, F. et al. Comparative transcriptome and metabolite analysis of oil palm and date palm mesocarp that differ dramatically in carbon partitioning. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 12527–12532 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1152">7</a></sup>, and found that 96.3%, 90.8% and 96.4% of the EST datasets are matched to the genome assembly, respectively. The reasons for the variable matching rates are largely platform-dependent, such as short read length and variable error rates (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#MOESM739">Supplementary Table S3</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome annotation</h3><p>We built a much larger pool of gene models for <i>P. dactylifera</i> than those previously reported, by combining <i>ab initio</i> predictions (Fgenesh++<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Solovyev, V., Kosarev, P., Seledsov, I. &amp; Vorobyev, D. Automatic annotation of eukaryotic genes, pseudogenes and promoters. Genome. Biol. 7 Suppl 1, S10 11–S10 12 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1173">12</a></sup>), EST assemblies (pyrosequencing data), RNA-seq reads (SOLiD data), plant protein-coding genes and protein domain information for gene annotation. The effort yielded 41,660 gene models (42,957 isoforms) in 10,363 scaffolds (472,329,057 bp in length; 84.6% of the total length) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#MOESM739">Supplementary Note 2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#MOESM739">Supplementary Table S4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#MOESM739">Supplementary Fig. S3</a>). Our proteome comparison of <i>P. dactylifera</i> to <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i>, <i>Sorghum bicolor</i> and <i>Vitis vinifera</i> revealed that 8,093 gene families are shared among all five plant genomes and 1,127 gene families are unique to <i>P. dactylifera</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="A Venn diagram showing the distribution of shared gene families among representative angiosperms."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: A Venn diagram showing the distribution of shared gene families among representative angiosperms.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3274/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3274/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3274_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3274/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3274_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="555" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><i>Phoenix dactylifera</i>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i>, <i>Sorghum bicolor</i> and <i>Vitis vinifera</i> (OrthoMCL 2.0.2, <i>E</i>&lt;1e-5). The number of gene families, the number of genes in the families and the total number of genes are indicated under species names, which are separated by slashes. A total of 12,624 sequences in 1,127 gene families are unique to <i>P. dactylifera,</i> and these unique gene families are mostly related to DNA/RNA metabolic process and ion binding (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#MOESM739">Supplementary Table S15</a>).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3274/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>There are two types of repetitive sequences in any given plant genomes: mathematically-defined and biologically-defined repeats<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Yu, J. et al. The genomes of Oryza sativa: a history of duplications. PLoS Biol. 3, e38 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1256">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Yu, J. et al. A draft sequence of the rice genome (Oryza sativa L. ssp. indica). Science 296, 79–92 (2002)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1259">14</a></sup>. In <i>P. dactylifera</i>, most biologically-defined repeats are retrotransposons, accounted for 21.99% of the genome, of which 14.03% and 4.17% are Ty1/Copia and Ty3/Gypsy, respectively. Annotated non-LTR retrotransposon LINEs and DNA transposons (hAT/Ac, CACTA/EnSpm and MITEs) make up merely 0.96% of the genome. Aside from ~2.15% microsatellite-derived mathematically-defined repeats (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#MOESM739">Supplementary Table S5</a>), the <i>P. dactylifera</i> genome harbours 38.41% repetitive sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#MOESM739">Supplementary Table S6</a>), a proportion that is within the range of other sequenced plant genomes, including <i>O. sativa</i> (39.5%), <i>V. vinifera</i> (41.4%) and <i>P. trichocarpa</i> (42%). Relying on the relative completeness of our genome assembly, we discovered Ty1/Copia has a much higher copy number than Ty3/Gypsy but in most other sequenced plant genomes the former has been found to be always less in number than the latter. Further analysis based on our in-house generated data from other palms confirmed that the pattern holds true for all palm family plants (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#MOESM739">Supplementary Table S7</a>). The most abundant Ty1/Copia elements in <i>P. dactylifera</i> show the highest homology to the rice retrotransposon element 1 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Noma, K., Nakajima, R., Ohtsubo, H. &amp; Ohtsubo, E. RIRE1, a retrotransposon from wild rice Oryza australiensis. Genes Genet. Syst. 72, 131–140 (1997)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1290">15</a>) in the conserved region of the reverse transcriptase genes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genome-wide duplication</h3><p>Genome-wide duplication (GWD) is rather prevalent among angiosperms that share the same ancestor ~150 Mya (refs <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="De Bodt, S., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Genome duplication and the origin of angiosperms. Trends Ecol. Evol. 20, 591–597 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1301">16</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Friis, E. M., Pedersen, K. R. &amp; Crane, P. R. Cretaceous angiosperm flowers: Innovation and evolution in plant reproduction. Palaeogeogr. Palaeoclimatol. Palaeoecol. 232, 251–293 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1304">17</a>). GWD provides essential genetic material for the creation of novel functions for adapting new environment and tolerating biotic and abiotic stresses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Freeling, M. Bias in plant gene content following different sorts of duplication: tandem, whole-genome, segmental, or by transposition. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 60, 433–453 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1308">18</a></sup>. Using 4,215 paralogous gene pairs in 411 collinear regions of the <i>P. dactylifera</i> genome assembly, we assessed the distribution of Ks or 4DTv to show two distinct peaks: Ks ~0.314 (4DTv~0.107) and Ks ~0.833 (4DTv~0.332) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#MOESM739">Supplementary Fig. S4</a>). After the exclusion of the recent GWD of <i>Z. mays</i> genome, the curve is similar to that of <i>P. trichocarpa</i> but not to those of Gramineae plants (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>); the first peak corresponds to a GWD event shared among all angiosperms and the second peak derives from either a single more ancient GWD or massive consecutive segmental duplications when the slow substitution rate of the palm family<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Wilson, M. A., Gaut, B. &amp; Clegg, M. T. Chloroplast DNA evolves slowly in the palm family (Arecaceae). Mol. Biol. Evol. 7, 303–314 (1990)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1328">19</a></sup> and abundant fossil records from ~90 Mya (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Harley, M. M. A summary of fossil records for Arecaceae. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 151, 39–67 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1331">20</a>) are both considered (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#MOESM739">Supplementary Note 3</a>). We also found significant macro-synteny between <i>P. dactylifera</i> and other monocotyledons (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#MOESM739">Supplementary Fig. S5</a>) but failed to observe it between <i>P. dactylifera</i> and any dicotyledons. Remarkably, the biggest scaffold, pdS00001 (~4.5 Mb in length), appears highly conserved and is part of the ‘concentric circles’ of monocotyledons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Abrouk, M. et al. Palaeogenomics of plants: synteny-based modelling of extinct ancestors. Trends. Plant. Sci. 15, 479–487 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1348">21</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Ks distribution of GWD in selected monocotyledons and dicotyledons."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Ks distribution of GWD in selected monocotyledons and dicotyledons.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3274/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3274/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3274_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3274/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3274_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="786" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>The density distribution of each monocotyledon (<b>a</b>) or dicotyledon (<b>b</b>) was calculated based on kernel density estimation with a bin size of 0.001. The evolutionary rate is relative slow in trees<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Gaut, B. S., Morton, B. R., McCaig, B. C. &amp; Clegg, M. T. Substitution rate comparisons between grasses and palms: synonymous rate differences at the nuclear gene Adh parallel rate differences at the plastid gene rbcL. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 93, 10274–10279 (1996)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1373">47</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Koch, M. A., Haubold, B. &amp; Mitchell-Olds, T. Comparative evolutionary analysis of chalcone synthase and alcohol dehydrogenase loci in Arabidopsis, Arabis, and related genera (Brassicaceae). Genome Biol. Evol. 17, 1483–1498 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1376">48</a></sup>, such as those of <i>P. trichocarpa</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i>. We suppose that the <i>P. dactylifera</i> genome also has this character similar to oil palm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Bourgis, F. et al. Comparative transcriptome and metabolite analysis of oil palm and date palm mesocarp that differ dramatically in carbon partitioning. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 12527–12532 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1390">7</a></sup>. The minor peak of <i>O. sativa</i> (Ks ~0.06) was recently suggested to be a GWD event happened ~70 Mya and the genome underwent an illegitimate recombination followed by fast evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Wang, X. Y., Tang, H. B. &amp; Paterson, A. H. Seventy million years of concerted evolution of a homoeologous chromosome pair, in parallel, in major Poaceae lineages. Plant Cell 23, 27–37 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1397">49</a></sup>. <i>Z. mays</i> had a recent GWD event (Ks~0.2, ~5–12 Mya) as compared with other Gramineae species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Schnable, P. S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112–1115 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1404">50</a></sup>. In <i>M. truncatula</i>, a peak near 0 (Ks~0.05) is due to local duplication and faster evolution rate as compared with other vascular plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Young, N. D. et al. The Medicago genome provides insight into the evolution of rhizobial symbioses. Nature 480, 520–524 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1412">51</a></sup>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3274/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2
