xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Macro-synteny among P. dactylifera scaffolds and other monocotyledon chromosomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Macro-synteny among <i>P. dactylifera</i> scaffolds and other monocotyledon chromosomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3274/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3274/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3274_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3274/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3274_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="644" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Scaffolds pdS00001 (4.5 M), pdS00072 (1.1 M), pdS00080 (1 M) pdS00086 (1 M) and pdS00147 (0.7 M) have the same syntenic pattern with other monocotyledons. In addition, the latter four scaffolds have syntenic regions with pdS00001 and are inferred to be duplicated at monocotyledon ancestry (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#MOESM739">Supplementary Fig. S5</a>). The unit of scale is million base pairs or Mb for other monocotyledon chromosomes. All <i>P. dactylifera</i> scaffolds are enlarged 30 × for illustration.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3274/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Expansion of the late embryogenesis abundant family</h3><p>Date palms thrive in arid regions of the Middle East and North Africa, armed with its resistances to abiotic stresses, including drought, salinity and heat. Despite possible roles of physiological mechanisms, expansion of resistance-related gene families in <i>P. dactylifera</i> is expected. We scrutinized the late embryogenesis abundant (LEA) gene family that has been classified into eight groups and 84 gene members across taxa (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#MOESM739">Supplementary Table S8</a>). The group 2 LEA (or LEA2) genes are of particular abundance in the <i>P. dactylifera</i> genome assembly; there are 62 LEA2 members in <i>P. dactylifera</i>, as compared with 52 and 46 in rice and sorghum, respectively. Corresponding to their individual member expansions, this <i>P. dactylifera</i> LEA family also shows a complex expression profile in different tissues/organs and developmental stages according to our transcriptomic data. Among 12 different samples, 67 LEA genes were detected as expressed, most of them in a tissue-/organ-specific manner (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>). In general, the subfamilies of LEA1, LEA3, LEA4, LEA5, LEA6, SMP and Dehydrin are seed associated or male flower associated, whereas the LEA2 members are nearly all ubiquitous. As some of the LEA genes have been demonstrated to enhance tolerances to desiccation, cold, salination and osmosis stresses in various plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Shao, H. B., Liang, Z. S. &amp; Shao, M. A. LEA proteins in higher plants: structure, function, gene expression and regulation. Colloids Surf. B Biointerfaces 45, 131–135 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1482">22</a></sup>, expansion of the <i>P. dactylifera</i> LEA2 may be responsible, at least in part, for the resistance to abiotic stresses.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Expression patterns of the LEA genes among 12 tissues of P. dactylifera."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Expression patterns of the LEA genes among 12 tissues of <i>P. dactylifera</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3274/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3274/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3274_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3274/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3274_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1173" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>The LEA subfamilies are colour-coded (on the right side). The expression levels are normalized based on the <i>Z</i>-score method (scale bar) over all libraries (on the top).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3274/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Defining differentially expressed genes</h3><p>Since fruit or date is the most economically valuable product of date palm, we made an effort to identify differentially expressed genes (DEGs) involved in fruit development and ripening to provide a starting point for further investigation. We identified 4,134 DEGs, whose expressions significantly vary among seven fruit developmental stages, using RNA-Seq data; (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#MOESM739">Supplementary Table S9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#MOESM739">Supplementary Data 1</a>). When clustering the DEGs into different groups (upregulated, downregulated, not-regulated group 1 and not-regulated group 2; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#MOESM739">Supplementary Fig. S6</a>), we found that the two major groups, the up-regulated and the downregulated, show different enrichments of DEGs—gluconeogenesis, cellular carbohydrate metabolism and small molecule biosynthesis in the upregulated group as opposed to biological regulation, transcription and regulation of RNA metabolic process in the downregulated group (hypergeometric <i>P</i>&lt;0.01, Bonferroni false discovery rate &lt;0.05; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#MOESM739">Supplementary Tables S10 and S11</a>). This result indicates that most of the molecular events are down-regulated in the late stage of fruit development except sugar accumulation resulting in unusually high sugar content in dates.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Kyoto encyclopedia of genes and genomes pathways</h3><p>Our analyses on transcriptomic data were focused on fruiting based on Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG) pathways. It is unequivocal that ‘carbohydrate metabolism’ is the most activated pathway at every single stage of the fruit development and ripening. ‘Energy metabolism’ and ‘metabolism of other amino acids’ are the second and the third highly expressed pathways, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#MOESM739">Supplementary Fig. S7a</a>). Within the ‘carbohydrate metabolism’ pathway, we observed that genes directly associated with sugar metabolism are much more activated at the later stages of fruiting and that their expressions peak at 120 or 135 days post pollination (DPP) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#MOESM739">Supplementary Fig. S7b</a>). Taking the ‘fructose and mannose metabolism pathway’ as an example, the gene expression of this pathway increases gradually and reaches its highest level at 120 DPP. This general pattern provides a possible explanation for the high fructose content in the dates. Many of the ‘energy metabolism’-related genes are also elevated at the later stages, similar to that in ‘carbohydrate metabolism’ (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#MOESM739">Supplementary Fig. S7c</a>). However, genes involved in photosynthesis are highly activated at the middle of the fruit development (75 DPP), when fruits remain green and their sizes are close to those of the mature form.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Sugar metabolism</h3><p>As dates are famed for their high sugar content, we constructed gene expression profiles of 30 sugar metabolic enzymes and clustered them into two essential groups: early expressed genes and late expressed genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#Fig5">Fig. 5a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#MOESM739">Supplementary Data 2</a>). We first compared gene expression between fruit and leaf, and noticed that ribulose-1,5-bisphosphate carboxylase and two other enzymes genes, pyruvate orthophosphate dikinase and fructose-1, 6-bisphosphatase I, which are involved in invertible catalyzed sucrose synthesis reaction, are expressed much higher in green leaves. This result indicates that most of the sucrose accumulation in fruit may be contributed from leaves. C4-carbon fixation enzyme, phosphoenolpyruvate carboxylase, appears upregulated gradually during fruiting, perhaps having an important role in supplying carbon source at the late fruiting stages. Most other carbon assimilation-associated genes show a similar pattern where higher expression is observed in fruits rather than in green leaves and this also holds true in oil palm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Bourgis, F. et al. Comparative transcriptome and metabolite analysis of oil palm and date palm mesocarp that differ dramatically in carbon partitioning. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 12527–12532 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1571">7</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#Fig5">Fig. 5a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#MOESM739">Supplementary Fig. S8</a>). These results provide evidence that date palm fruit or the date is also involved in carbon fixation or refixation reaction during fruit development and produces pyruvate, glycerate-3-P and sucrose for both glycolysis and sugar accumulation. We also looked into sucrose metabolism and the expression profiles of its three key enzymes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Hesse, H. &amp; Willmitzer, L. Expression analysis of a sucrose synthase gene from sugar beet (Beta vulgaris L). Plant Mol. Biol. 30, 863–872 (1996)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1582">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Huber, S. C. &amp; Huber, J. L. Role and regulation of sucrose-phosphate synthase in higher plants. Annu. Rev. Plant Phys. 47, 431–444 (1996)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1585">24</a></sup>: sucrose synthase, sucrose-phosphate synthase and invertase (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#Fig5">Fig. 5a</a>). The transcriptions of <i>sps</i> and <i>inv</i>, not <i>sus</i>, show higher levels at the late stage, which agree with a previous observation on sugar content in date palm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Myhara, R. M., Karkalas, J. &amp; Taylor, M. S. The composition of maturing Omani dates. J. Sci. Food Agr. 79, 1345–1350 (1999)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1601">25</a></sup> and the key role assigned to sucrose-phosphate synthase, observed in citrus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Komatsu, A., Takanokura, Y., Moriguchi, T., Omura, M. &amp; Akihama, T. Differential expression of three sucrose-phosphate synthase isoforms during sucrose accumulation in citrus fruits (Citrus unshiu Marc.). Plant Sci. 140, 169–178 (1999)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1606">26</a></sup>, sugarcane<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Verma, A. K., Upadhyay, S. K., Verma, P. C., Solomon, S. &amp; Singh, S. B. Functional analysis of sucrose phosphate synthase (SPS) and sucrose synthase (SS) in sugarcane (Saccharum) cultivars. Plant Biol. 13, 325–332 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1610">27</a></sup> and banana<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Choudhury, S. R., Roy, S. &amp; Sengupta, D. N. A comparative study of cultivar differences in sucrose phosphate synthase gene expression and sucrose formation during banana fruit ripening. Postharvest Biol. Technol. 54, 15–24 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1614">28</a></sup>. The increasing expression of <i>inv</i> also supports the mechanism where invertase splits sucrose into glucose and fructose, leading to the final fructose deposition in dates. Of particular interest is the notion that starch hydrolysis and carbon fixation pathways are both switched on, and related genes are highly expressed at 120 DPP. These two pathways also contribute to the glucose and fructose accumulation in dates (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#Fig5">Fig. 5b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Expression profiling of sugar metabolism-related genes at different fruiting stages."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5: Expression profiling of sugar metabolism-related genes at different fruiting stages.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3274/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3274/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3274_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3274/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3274_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="731" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Gene expression profiling of 30 key enzymes related to sugar metabolism. The hierarchical clustering shows that these genes can be classified into two patterns: genes expressed at the early developmental stages (EE, blue, 0–75 DPP) and at the late developmental stages (LE, red, 105–135 DPP). (<b>b</b>) Summary of sugar metabolisms during fruit development and ripening. Fruits at 150 DPP are completely ripe and we were unable to extract enough RNA for RNA-seq.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3274/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Genetic diversity among varieties</h3><p>On genetic diversity, <i>P. dactylifera</i> is unique in several counts. First, it is a long-lived vegetatively propagated (offshoots are often used) species similar to poplar, having potential to contribute gametes to multiple generations in that a single genotype can persist in a garden for millennia. Second, a single phenotype, always female, can be selected as a clone to grow for further cultivation and selection. Third, the history of a date palm variety may be more complex than one anticipates—being carried for unusually long distance, cross-bred very intensively, and selected very heavily. As the <i>Khalas</i> genome assembly has an exceedingly high coverage of 122 × , we were able to define its allelic differences and calculated its single-nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) and indel densities as 2.57 and 0.10 per kb, respectively. We also identified the SNP (indel) densities for <i>Agwa</i>, <i>Fahal</i> and <i>Skury</i>, which are 6.10 (0.25), 5.51 (0.15) and 6.24 (0.20) per kb, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#MOESM739">Supplementary Tables S12 and S13</a>). Although sequence assemblies of seven other varieties are also publically available<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Al-Dous, E. K. et al. De novo genome sequencing and comparative genomics of date palm (Phoenix dactylifera). Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 521–527 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1677">6</a></sup>, the genome coverage of these assemblies is rather low for short read-based sequence mapping, ranging from 12 × to 39 × . On the basis of our analysis, their SNP densities range from 3.85 per kb to 6.63 per kb, which are slightly higher than what expected but not very far off from the range of high-coverage assemblies.</p><p>Chromosomal regions with reduced genetic diversity or ‘SNP deserts’ are often observed and linked to beneficial mutations that are often related to strong positive selection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Kaplan, N. L., Hudson, R. R. &amp; Langley, C. H. The hitchhiking effect revisited. Genetics 123, 887–899 (1989)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1684">29</a></sup>. Over the past decade, several studies on SNP deserts have been reported in both mammals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Cervino, A. C. L. et al. A comprehensive mouse IBD database for the efficient localization of quantitative trait loci. Mamm. Genome 17, 565–574 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1688">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Thomas, G. et al. A multistage genome-wide association study in breast cancer identifies two new risk alleles at 1p11.2 and 14q24.1 (RAD51L1). Nat. Genet. 41, 579–584 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1691">31</a></sup> and plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Yu, J., Wong, G. K. S., Liu, S. Q., Wang, J. A. &amp; Yang, H. M. A comprehensive crop genome research project: the superhybrid rice genome project in China. Phil. Trans. R. Soc. B 362, 1023–1034 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1695">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Wang, L. et al. SNP deserts of Asian cultivated rice: genomic regions under domestication. J. Evol. Biol. 22, 751–761 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1698">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="He, Z. W. et al. Two evolutionary histories in the genome of rice: the roles of domestication genes. PLoS Genet. 7, e1002100 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1701">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Xu, X. et al. Resequencing 50 accessions of cultivated and wild rice yields markers for identifying agronomically important genes. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 105–U157 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1704">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Austin, R. S. et al. Next-generation mapping of Arabidopsis genes. Plant J. 67, 715–725 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1707">36</a></sup>. Our previous study found that agronomically important QTLs, such as MADS-box and the Waxy genes, are located in rice SNP deserts<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Yu, J., Wong, G. K. S., Liu, S. Q., Wang, J. A. &amp; Yang, H. M. A comprehensive crop genome research project: the superhybrid rice genome project in China. Phil. Trans. R. Soc. B 362, 1023–1034 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1711">32</a></sup>. A detailed statistical analysis also revealed positive selections on functional genes and degenerative effects on gene expression in SNP deserts that are closely related to domestication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Wang, L. et al. SNP deserts of Asian cultivated rice: genomic regions under domestication. J. Evol. Biol. 22, 751–761 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1715">33</a></sup>. It was reported that low-SNP regions or SNP deserts in the cultivated rice are presumably attributable to domestication processes that include artificial selection, population size reduction, introgression and selfing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="He, Z. W. et al. Two evolutionary histories in the genome of rice: the roles of domestication genes. PLoS Genet. 7, e1002100 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1720">34</a></sup>, and thousands of candidate genes have also been identified for rice domestication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Xu, X. et al. Resequencing 50 accessions of cultivated and wild rice yields markers for identifying agronomically important genes. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 105–U157 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1724">35</a></sup>. On the basis of these discoveries, we investigated how selection has left its signatures in genomes of different date palm varieties by first evaluating the distribution of SNPs on large scaffolds and subsequently pinning down functional genes that are obviously selected, especially those closely involved in sugar and energy metabolisms. Clearly, SNPs/indels are not randomly distributed but exhibit a bimodal curve (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#Fig6">Fig. 6</a>), where the low-SNP peak (&lt;1 SNP per kb in a minimal length of 10 kb, about 18% of the genome assembly; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#MOESM739">Supplementary Fig. S9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#MOESM739">Supplementary Table S14</a>) reflects trait selection in cultivation and thus considered as the signature of domestication, and this result is consistent with the previous reports in rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Yu, J., Wong, G. K. S., Liu, S. Q., Wang, J. A. &amp; Yang, H. M. A comprehensive crop genome research project: the superhybrid rice genome project in China. Phil. Trans. R. Soc. B 362, 1023–1034 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1737">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Wang, L. et al. SNP deserts of Asian cultivated rice: genomic regions under domestication. J. Evol. Biol. 22, 751–761 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1740">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="He, Z. W. et al. Two evolutionary histories in the genome of rice: the roles of domestication genes. PLoS Genet. 7, e1002100 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1743">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Xu, X. et al. Resequencing 50 accessions of cultivated and wild rice yields markers for identifying agronomically important genes. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 105–U157 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1746">35</a></sup>. The SNP frequency distributions appear to have two different patterns among the varieties. The most popular commercial varieties, <i>Khalas</i>, <i>Medjool</i> and <i>Deglet Noor</i>, together with their backcrossed descendants, <i>KhalsFx</i> (<i>Khalas</i> × <i>Khalas</i> F1), <i>Medjool</i> BC4 and <i>Deglet Noor</i> BC5, have a more obvious bimodal SNP distribution. In contrast, the distribution of non-commercial varieties <i>AlrijalF</i> and <i>Fahal</i> only have the major peak. Furthermore, the backcrossed descendants should have higher proportion of SNP desert regions as compared with their recurrent female parents (<i>Deglet Noor</i> and <i>Medjool</i>), as backcrossing is known to reduce genetic effect by one-half at each generation theoretically, and the reason why backcrossed <i>P. dactylifera</i> descendants are more conserved than their recurrent parents<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Al-Dous, E. K. et al. De novo genome sequencing and comparative genomics of date palm (Phoenix dactylifera). Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 521–527 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d54214e1792">6</a></sup> may be related to both trait selection and artificial pollination (usually by hands and using commercial pollens with ambiguous origin).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Phylogenetic analysis of 11 P. dactylifera varieties based on SNPs and their SNPs frequency distributions."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 6: Phylogenetic analysis of 11 <i>P. dactylifera</i> varieties based on SNPs and their SNPs frequency distributions.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3274/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3274/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3274_Fig6_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms3274/MediaObjects/41467_2013_Article_BFncomms3274_Fig6_HTML.jpg" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="870" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>The phylogenetic tree was constructed by using all SNP sites of the varieties (NJ method with 1,000 bootstrap, MEGA 5.0). The series of plots show SNP density (SNP per kb, the horizontal axis) and frequencies (the vertical axis). SNP frequency was calculated based on a 10-kb sliding window in 1-kb steps. The modes of SNP distribution are compounding, where the minor mode is contributed by SNP deserts.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms3274/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>As compared with that of the whole genome, the gene/repeat ratio of SNP deserts is over 3 × higher (3.12 versus 1.00; Pearson’s <i>χ</i><sup>2</sup> value=13.541, <i>P</i>=0.000233). Non-synonymous SNPs are also enriched in SNP deserts (1.76 versus 1.18; Pearson’s <i>χ</i><sup>2</sup> value=60.47, <i>P</i>=0). Furthermore, we also calculated gene density in SNP deserts and the whole genome by defining gene density as Ds gene per kb: 0.083 for SNP deserts (9,036 genes in 108,259 kb) and 0.069 for the genome average (41,660 genes in 605,351 kb). This result suggests that gene density is higher in SNP deserts as opposed to the genome average and it is further supported by the notion that there are also a higher proportion of gene families in SNP deserts. In addition, we found that SNP deserts tend to recruit genes associated with abiotic/disease resistances and energy/sugar metabolisms (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#Tab2">Table 2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Genes and gene families enriched in SNP deserts.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ncomms3274/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The extremely broad geographic distribution of <i>P. dactylifera</i> implies that it either has evolved as a plant covering extensive soil and climatic conditions or spreads with help from humans after having originated from a more limited geographic region. Owing to rather limited sampling, it is very difficult for us to draw a clear picture of the actual origin of date palm but we noticed that the northern African varieties, <i>Deglet Noor</i> and <i>Medjool</i>, are significantly diverged from the Middle Eastern varieties, as Saudi Arabian varieties (<i>Khalas</i>, <i>Agwa</i>, <i>Fahal</i> and <i>Sukry</i>) are clustered into a single clade (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms3274#Fig6">Fig. 6</a>). These results indicate that geographic isolation and artificial selection may both contribute to the diversity and evolution of <i>P. dactylifera</i>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><p>Plant genomics is now entering a new era when genome assemblies are being produced based on complementary data from different platforms where high-coverage and satisfactory contiguity can both be achieved. Our initial analysis of the <i>P. dactylifera</i> genome provides a detailed view on genome-wide structural parameters of genes, histories of genome/gene duplications, genetic diversities of cultivar resources and functional genes in key functional categories. There are other important remaining issues for further exploitations. First, the current <i>P. dactylifera</i> genome assembly is much improved in both precision and contiguity, allowing us to position the genome in an evolutionary context by carrying out comparative studies between and among species and varieties. Second, because <i>P. dactylifera</i> is an obligate out-crossing species and its recessive alleles tend to be maintained in a heterozygous state, as we already observed in <i>Khalas</i>, more <i>P. dactylifera</i> varieties should be sequenced to a high coverage for quantitative trait mapping. Third, as we observed, the palm family seems to have a unique ratio of common retrotransposons so that it is desirable to classify and date them to investigate the possible reasons as to how repeats of different types may relate to genome dynamics, such as the generation of new genes. Fourth, high-quality sequence assemblies are of essence for better genome annotation for plants, as plant genomes are often large and structured very differently from animal genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Yu, J., Wong, G. K.-S., Wang, J. &amp; Yang, H. inEncyclopedia of Molecular Cell Biology and Molecular Medicine Vol. 13, ed. Meyers Robert A. 71–114Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d54214e2180">37</a></sup>. Fifth, many biological features unique to date palm or the palm family trees in general, such as fruit and nutrition content diversities, are to be studied in greater details at molecular levels, providing better knowledge for improving date quality and yield.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec12" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Materials</h3><p>We collected <i>P. dactylifera</i> tissue samples (green leaves, yellow leaves, flowers, fruits, offshoots, and roots) for the following varieties: <i>Khalas</i> (female) and <i>Fahal</i> (male) samples from a date palm farm in Al-Hssa Oasis (25°04′35″N, 49°06′24″E), and <i>Sukry</i> (female) and <i>Agwa</i> (female) samples from Al-Qassim and Al-Medina Al-Monwarh, respectively, in Saudi Arabia. The fruit samples in various developmental stages of <i>Khalas</i> were harvested from a date palm farm at Al-Kharj (24°08′54″N, 47°18′18″E), Saudi Arabia. After thorough washing with double-distilled water, we immediately froze the samples in liquid nitrogen and transported them to the laboratory on dry ice. The samples were stored inside −80 °C freezers until use.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">DNA isolation</h3><p>Our DNA extraction protocol combines the high-molecular-weight DNA extraction<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Zhang, H. B., Zhao, X. P., Ding, X. L., Paterson, A. H. &amp; Wing, R. A. Preparation of megabase-size DNA from plant nuclei. Plant J. 7, 175–184 (1995)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d54214e2223">38</a></sup> and hexadecyl trimethyl ammonium bromide (CTAB) method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Porebski, S., Bailey, L. G. &amp; Baum, B. R. Modification of a CTAB DNA extraction protocol for plants containing high polysaccharide and polyphenol components. Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 15, 8–15 (1997)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d54214e2227">39</a></sup> for improved removal of polysaccharides and other contaminations. The date palm tissues were ground into a fine powder in the presence of liquid nitrogen and subsequently homogenized in HB buffer (10 mM Tris–HCl, 80 mM KCl, 10 mM EDTA, 1 mM spermidine, 0.5 mM spermine, 0.5% β-mercaptoethanol, 0.5 M Sucrose, pH 9.4). After several rounds of centrifugation at 800 × <i>g</i> to remove tissue residues, the nucleus-containing pellets were collected after centrifugation at 2,000 × <i>g</i> for 10 min. The nuclei were broken by osmotic pressure in CTAB lysis solution (2% CTAB, 1.4 M NaCl, 0.1 M Tris–HCl at pH 8.0, 0.02 M EDTA at pH 8.0) at 70 °C for 45 min. After a phenol–chloroform extraction, DNA samples were precipitated using precooled isopropanol and washed twice with 70% ethanol.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">RNA extraction</h3><p>Total RNA was extracted from 5 g of plant material by grinding it in liquid nitrogen and it was subsequently dissolved in 20 ml of preheated extraction buffer at 65 °C (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Chang, S. J., Puryear, J. &amp; Cairney, J. A simple and efficient method for isolating RNA from pine trees. Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 11, 113–116 (1993)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d54214e2244">40</a>). The RNA sample was extracted twice with equal volumes of chloroform: isoamyl alcohol (24:1) in the presence of a 1/4 volume of 10 M LiCl and precipitated overnight at 4 °C. The RNA pellet was collected via ethanol precipitation and dissolved in 500 μl of SSTE (sodium dodecyl sulfate–Tris–HCl–EDTA) buffer. After futher extraction with equal volume of chloroform: isoamyl alcohol (24:1), the purified RNA was precipitated and treated with DNase I.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Shotgun library construction and sequencing</h3><p>For Roche/454 data acquisition, 5 μg of genomic DNA was sheared into 500–800 bp fragments with a Nebulizer and both DNA ends were filled enzymatically. Library construction and the emPCR were performed according to the GS FLX Titanium General Library Preparation Guide and emPCR Method Manual (Roche), respectively. For the SOLiD data acquisition, 20 μg or more DNA (depending on insert size) samples were used to construct LMP libraries, according to the SOLiD 4 System Library Preparation Guide (Life Technologies), and the cDNA was sheared into fragments (1–8 kb) with hydroshear. After end-filling, the DNA fragments were ligated to the LMP adaptors and circularized. For RNA-seq, we used the RiboMinus Plant Kit for RNA-Seq (Life Technologies) to remove the ribosomal RNA from DNase I-treated total RNA. The starting material was 7 μg of total RNA, which yielded ~700 ng of RiboMinus RNA (rmRNA). Two hundred nanograms of rmRNA was used for the transcriptome library construction according to the SOLiD Total RNA-Seq Kit protocol.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">BAC DNA extraction and BAC-end sequencing</h3><p>The <i>P. dactylifera</i> BAC library was constructed (by Amplicon Express, Washington) with <i>Hin</i>dIII- and-<i>Eco</i>RI-digested DNA and the pCC1BAC vector (Epicentre). The quality of the library was assessed by using restriction digestion with <i>Hin</i>dIII of 28 randomly picked clones (116 kb to 131 kb). A total of 110,592 clones were randomly picked and stored in 384-well plates. For BAC-end sequencing, we extracted DNA from a 1 ml culture using an alkaline lysis protocol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Birnboim, H. &amp; Doly, J. A rapid alkaline extraction procedure for screening recombinant plasmid DNA. Nucleic Acids Res. 7, 1513–1523 (1979)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d54214e2277">41</a></sup> in a 96-well filter plate (Thermo Fisher). Two micrograms of BAC DNA was used for the sequencing reaction with the BigDye Terminator v3.1 Cycle Sequencing Kit (Life Technologies).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Genome assembly</h3><p>The backbone of our genome assembly was largely based on pyrosequencing reads. First, we assembled quality-filtered reads to build contigs using the default settings of Newbler (version 2.5.3). Second, we mapped the SOLiD mate-pair tags using BioScope (version 1.3) to the Newbler consensus contigs and estimated gap length according to insert sizes of the mate-pair libraries using a SOLiD-specific tool called HAPS (version 0.1.200; <a href="http://solidsoftwaretools.com/gf/project/haps/">http://solidsoftwaretools.com/gf/project/haps/</a>) with the linked read number ≥10. After filtering out short and repeat-containing contigs, we used the unique contigs (≥500 bp) to build super-contigs. Third, we filled gaps in the super-contigs by using both the Newbler contigs and pyrosequencing reads with a minimal 15-bp matched end. Finally, we used the BACs whose two end sequences were properly aligned to the super-contigs to construct the final scaffolds. The genome annotation files, mapping results of date palm varieties, mapping results of transcriptome data, and the assembled full-length cDNA sequences are available at the JCGR website ( <a href="http://www.kacst.edu.sa/en/depts/jcg/home/Pages/default.aspx">http://www.kacst.edu.sa/en/depts/jcg/home/Pages/default.aspx</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">LEA gene identification</h3><p>We searched LEA domains (Pfam with <i>E</i> value ≤1e-5) against the predicted <i>P. dactylifera</i> gene models and other sequenced plant genes/genomes using HMMER v3.0 and validated the candidate LEA genes using Blastp against plant LEA protein sequences of UniProt ( <a href="http://www.uniprot.org/">http://www.uniprot.org/</a>). We aligned the SOLiD RNA-seq data (12 different tissues/organs and &gt;40 million reads for each library) to the candidate LEA genes and the mapped read number was normalized based on the <i>Z</i>-score method for heatmap (heatmap2 of R package).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Fruit-ripening-related gene expression</h3><p>We mapped the SOLiD RNA-Seq reads generated from <i>P. dactylifera</i> samples at seven distinct fruit developmental stages (0, 15, 30, 60, 90, 120 and 135 DPP) to the gene models using Bioscope v1.3 with default parameters. The uniquely mapped reads for gene expression were defined with a threshold of &gt;5 and normalized to per kb of genes per million reads (RPKM)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Mortazavi, A., Williams, B. A., McCue, K., Schaeffer, L. &amp; Wold, B. Mapping and quantifying mammalian transcriptomes by RNA-Seq. Nat. Methods 5, 621–628 (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d54214e2338">42</a></sup>. We annotated genes using InterProScan, KAAS (KAAS, <a href="http://www.genome.ad.jp/tools/kaas/">http://www.genome.ad.jp/tools/kaas/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Moriya, Y., Itoh, M., Okuda, S., Yoshizawa, A. C. &amp; Kanehisa, M. KAAS: an automatic genome annotation and pathway reconstruction server. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W182–W185 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d54214e2349">43</a></sup> and KEGG basic metabolic pathway. We identified DEGs during fruit ripening using edgeR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Robinson, M. D., McCarthy, D. J. &amp; Smyth, G. K. edgeR: a Bioconductor package for differential expression analysis of digital gene expression data. Bioinformatics 26, 139–140 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d54214e2353">44</a></sup> and clustered all them based on RPKM values using the R hclust function. We also conducted gene ontology enrichment analysis of the two major clusters using AGRIGO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Du, Z., Zhou, X., Ling, Y., Zhang, Z. H. &amp; Su, Z. agriGO: a GO analysis toolkit for the agricultural community. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, W64–W70 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d54214e2358">45</a></sup>. On the basis of gene annotation and transcriptome analysis, we estimated gene expression levels of each pathway in the KEGG ‘metabolism’ category according to the following protocol. First, expression levels for each KEGG ortholog (KO) were calculated as the sum of all RPKM values for each gene. This value at seven distinct fruit development stages was defined as the KO expression value. All KO expression values in a single pathway were then averaged, giving the pathway expression value. Finally, the average value of pathway expression values for each subcategory of the KEGG ‘metabolism’ category was calculated.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Diversity analysis of date palm varieties</h3><p>We mapped the SOLiD mate-pair reads from <i>Khalas</i>, <i>Agwa</i>, <i>Sukry</i> and <i>Fahal</i> (male) to the our (<i>Khalas</i>) genome assembly and called sequence variations both between the two alleles of a genome and among all genomes using Pileup, a software package of SAM tool<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078–2079 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms3274#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d54214e2386">46</a></sup>. To reduce false positives, we filtered the SNPs and indels using total read coverage ≥4, frequency of SNPs or indels ≥20%, and observed SNPs or indels ≥2 as thresholds. We also used raw sequencing reads of <i>AlrijalF</i>, <i>Deglet Noor</i>, <i>Deglet Noor BC5</i>, <i>Khalt</i>, <i>KhalsFx</i>, <i>Medjool</i> and <i>Medjool BC4</i> from the SRA database for a combined analysis.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec22" data-title="Additional information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec22-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec22">Additional information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec22-content"><p><b>Accession codes:</b> Date palm genome assembly, BAC clones, and transcriptomic data were deposited in Genbank with BioProject ID PRJNA83433, and the EST raw reads were submitted to Sequence Read Archive (SRA; <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRA045434">SRA045434</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRA049307">SRA049307</a> and <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRX096040">SRX096040</a>).</p><p><b>How to cite this article:</b> Al-Mssallem, I. S. <i>et al.</i> Genome sequence of the date palm <i>Phoenix dactylifera</i> L. <i>Nat. Commun.</i> 4:2274 doi: 10.1038/ncomms3274 (2013).</p></div></div></section>
